<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After two centuries, the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">policy of seclusion</a> under the <i><a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dgun" title="Shōgun">shōguns</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> came to an end when the country was opened to trade by the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Kanagawa" title="Convention of Kanagawa">Convention of Kanagawa</a> in 1854. In the years following the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> of 1868 and the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">shogunate</a>, the newly formed Meiji government embarked on reforms to centralize and modernize Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2002343_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2002343-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese had sent delegations and students around the world to learn and assimilate <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western</a> arts and sciences, with the intention of making Japan an equal to the Western powers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2002335_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2002335-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> These reforms transformed Japan from a <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> society into a modern industrial state.
</p><p>The Qing Dynasty had also started to undergo reform in both military and political doctrine, but was far from successful.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_politics">Korean politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Korean politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In January 1864, <a href="/wiki/Cheoljong_of_Joseon" title="Cheoljong of Joseon">Cheoljong of Joseon</a> died without a male heir, and through Korean succession protocols <a href="/wiki/Gojong_of_Korea" title="Gojong of Korea">Gojong of Korea</a> ascended the throne at the age of 12. However, as King Gojong was too young to rule, the new king's father, Yi Ha-ŭng, became the <a href="/wiki/Heungseon_Daewongun" title="Heungseon Daewongun">Heungseon Daewongun</a>, or lord of the great court, and ruled Korea in his son's name as regent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012279_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012279-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Originally the term Daewongun referred to any person who was not actually the king but whose son took the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012279_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012279-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> With his ascendancy to power the Daewongun initiated a set of reforms designed to strengthen the monarchy at the expense of the <a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">Yangban</a> class. He also pursued an isolationist policy and was determined to purge the kingdom of any foreign ideas that had infiltrated into the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012281_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012281-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In Korean history, the king's in-laws enjoyed great power, consequently the Daewongun acknowledged that any future daughters-in-law might threaten his authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012284_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012284-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, he attempted to prevent any possible threat to his rule by selecting as a new queen for his son an orphaned girl from among the <a href="/wiki/Yeoheung_Min_clan" title="Yeoheung Min clan">Yŏhŭng Min clan</a>, which lacked powerful political connections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012285_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012285-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Empress_Myeongseong" title="Empress Myeongseong">Empress Myeongseong</a> as his daughter-in-law and the royal consort, the Daewongun felt secure in his power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012285_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012285-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> However, after she had become queen, Min recruited all her relatives and had them appointed to influential positions in the name of the king. The Queen also allied herself with political enemies of the Daewongun, so that by late 1873 she had mobilized enough influence to oust him from power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012285_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012285-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In October 1873, when the Confucian scholar <a href="/wiki/Choe_Ik-hyeon" title="Choe Ik-hyeon">Choe Ik-hyeon</a> submitted a memorial to King Gojong urging him to rule in his own right, Queen Min seized the opportunity to force her father-in-law's retirement as regent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012285_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012285-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The departure of the Daewongun led to Korea's abandonment of its isolationist policy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012285_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012285-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opening_of_Korea">Opening of Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Opening of Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On February 26, 1876, after <a href="/wiki/Ganghwa_Island_incident" title="Ganghwa Island incident">confrontations</a> between the Japanese and Koreans, the <a href="/wiki/Japan%E2%80%93Korea_Treaty_of_1876" title="Japan–Korea Treaty of 1876">Ganghwa Treaty</a> was signed, opening Korea to Japanese trade. In 1880, the King sent a mission to Japan that was headed by <a href="/wiki/Kim_Hong-jip" title="Kim Hong-jip">Kim Hong-jip</a>, an enthusiastic observer of the reforms taking place there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011234_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011234-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> While in Japan, the Chinese diplomat Huang Zunxian presented him with a study called <i>"Chaoxian Celue"</i> (<i>A Strategy for Korea</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011234_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011234-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It warned of the threat to Korea posed by the Russians and recommended that Korea maintain friendly relations with Japan, which was at the time too economically weak to be an immediate threat, to work closely with China, and seek an alliance with the United States as a counterweight to Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011234–235_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011234–235-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> After returning to Korea, Kim presented the document to King Gojong, who was so impressed with the document that he had copies made and distributed to his officials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011235_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011235-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1880, following Chinese advice and breaking with tradition, King Gojong decided to establish diplomatic ties with the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012287_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012287-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> After negotiations through Chinese mediation in Tianjin, the <a href="/wiki/United_States%E2%80%93Korea_Treaty_of_1882" class="mw-redirect" title="United States–Korea Treaty of 1882">Treaty of Peace, Amity, Commerce, and Navigation</a> was formally signed between the United States and Korea in Incheon on May 22, 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012287_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012287-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, there were two significant issues raised by the treaty, the first concerned Korea's status as an independent nation. During the talks with the Americans, the Chinese insisted that the treaty contain an article declaring that Korea was a dependency of China and argued that the country had long been a tributary state of China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012287_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012287-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> But the Americans firmly opposed such an article, arguing that a treaty with Korea should be based on the Treaty of Ganghwa, which stipulated that Korea was an independent state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012288_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012288-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> A compromise was finally reached, with Shufeldt and Li agreeing that the King of Korea would notify the U.S president in a letter that Korea had special status as a tributary state of China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012288_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012288-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The treaty between the Korean government and the United States became the model for all treaties between it and other Western countries. Korea later signed similar trade and commerce treaties with Great Britain and Germany in 1883, with Italy and Russia in 1884, and with France in 1886. Subsequently, commercial treaties were concluded with other European countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012289_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012289-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_reforms">Korean reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Korean reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After 1879, China's relations with Korea came under the authority of Li Hongzhang, who had emerged as one of the most influential figures in China after playing an important role during the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a>, and was also an advocate of the <a href="/wiki/Self-strengthening_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-strengthening movement">self-strengthening movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011235_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011235-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In 1879, Li was appointed as governor-general of <a href="/wiki/Zhili_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhili Province">Zhili Province</a> and the imperial commissioner for the northern ports. He was in charge of China's Korea policy and urged Korean officials to adopt China's own self-strengthening program to strengthen their country in response to foreign threats, to which King Gojong was receptive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011235_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011235-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The Korean government, immediately after opening of the country to the outside world, pursued a policy of enlightenment aimed at achieving national prosperity and military strength through the doctrine of <i>tongdo sŏgi</i> (<i>Eastern ways and Western machines</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012289_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012289-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> To modernize their country, the Koreans tried selectively to accept and master Western technology while preserving their country's cultural values and heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012289_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012289-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1881, the government launched administrative reforms and established the <i>T'ongni kimu amun</i> (<i>Office for Extraordinary State Affairs</i>) which was modeled on Chinese administrative structures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012289_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012289-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Under this overarching organization, 12 <i>sa</i> or agencies were created.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012289_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012289-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In 1881, a technical mission was sent to Japan to survey its modernized facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012290_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012290-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Officials traveled all over Japan inspecting administrative, military, educational, and industrial facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012290_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012290-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In October, another small group went to Tianjin to study modern weapons manufacturing, and Chinese technicians were invited to manufacture weapons in Seoul. Additionally, as part of their plan to modernize the country, the Koreans had invited the Japanese military attaché Lieutenant Horimoto Reizō to serve as an adviser in creating a modern army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002372_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002372-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> A new military formation called the <i><a href="/wiki/Pyolgigun" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyolgigun">Pyŏlgigun</a></i> (<i>Special Skills Force</i>) was established, in which eighty to one hundred young men<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012289Keene2002373_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012289Keene2002373-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> of the aristocracy were to be given Japanese military training.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The following year, in January 1882, the government also reorganized the existing five-army garrison structure into the <i>Muwiyŏng</i> (<i>Palace Guards Garrison</i>) and the <i>Changŏyŏng</i> (<i>Capital Guards Garrison</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012289_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012289-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_insecurities_over_Korea">Japanese insecurities over Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Japanese insecurities over Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 1880s, discussions in Japan about national security focused on the issue of Korean reform. The political discourse over the two were interlinked, as the German military adviser Major <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Meckel" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacob Meckel">Jacob Meckel</a> stated, Korea was <i>"a dagger pointed at the heart of Japan"</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199849_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199849-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> What made Korea of strategic concern was not merely its proximity to Japan but its inability to defend itself against outsiders. If Korea were truly independent, it posed no strategic problem to Japan's national security but if the country remained backward and uncivilized it would remain weak and consequently would be inviting prey for foreign domination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199851_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199851-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The political consensus in Japan was that Korean independence lay, as it had been for Meiji Japan, through the importation of <i>"civilization"</i> from the West.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199849_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199849-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Korea required a program of self-strengthening like the post-Restoration reforms that were enacted in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199851_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199851-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese interest in the reform of Korea was not purely altruistic. Not only would these reforms enable Korea to resist foreign intrusion, which was in Japan's direct interest, but through being a conduit of change they would also have opportunity to play a larger role on the peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199849_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199849-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> To Meiji leaders, the issue was not whether Korea should be reformed but how these reforms might be implemented. There was a choice of adopting a passive role which required the cultivation of reformist elements within Korean society and rendering them assistance whenever possible, or adopting a more aggressive policy, actively interfering in Korean politics to assure that reform took place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199852_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199852-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Many Japanese advocates of Korean reform swung between these two positions.
</p><p>Japan in the early 1880s was weak, as a result of internal peasant uprisings and samurai rebellions during the previous decade. The country was also struggling financially, with inflation as a result of these internal factors. Subsequently, the Meiji government adopted a passive policy, encouraging the Korean court to follow the Japanese model but offering little concrete assistance except for the dispatch of the small military mission headed by Lieutenant Horimoto Reizo to train the <i>Pyŏlgigun</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199852_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199852-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> What worried the Japanese was the Chinese, who had loosened their hold over Korea in 1876 when the Japanese succeeded in establishing a legal basis for Korean independence by ending its tributary status.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199850_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199850-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Chinese actions appeared to be thwarting the forces of reform in Korea and re-asserting their influence over the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199850_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199850-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1882_crisis">1882 crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1882 crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Imo_Incident" title="Imo Incident">Imo Incident</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flight_of_Japanese_Legation_1882.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Flight_of_Japanese_Legation_1882.jpg/270px-Flight_of_Japanese_Legation_1882.jpg" decoding="async" width="270" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Flight_of_Japanese_Legation_1882.jpg/405px-Flight_of_Japanese_Legation_1882.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Flight_of_Japanese_Legation_1882.jpg/540px-Flight_of_Japanese_Legation_1882.jpg 2x" data-file-width="705" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flight_of_Japanese_Legation_1882.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-E" class="mw-redirect" title="Ukiyo-E">Woodblock print</a> depicting the flight of the Japanese legation in 1882</div></div></div>
<p>In 1882, the Korean Peninsula experienced a severe drought which led to food shortages, causing much hardship and discord among the population. Korea was on the verge of bankruptcy, even falling months behind on military pay, causing deep resentment among the soldiers. There was also resentment towards the <i>Pyŏlgigun</i> on the part of the soldiers of the regular Korean army, as the formation was better equipped and treated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002372_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002372-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, more than 1000 soldiers had been discharged in the process of overhauling the army, most of them were either old or disabled and the rest had not been given their pay in rice for thirteen months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June of that year, King Gojong, being informed of the situation, ordered that a month's allowance of rice be given to the soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He directed Min Gyeom-ho, the overseer of government finances and the Queen Min's nephew,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012282_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012282-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> to handle the matter. Min in turn handed the matter over to his steward who sold the good rice he had been given and used the money to buy millet which he mixed with sand and bran.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the rice became rotten and inedible. The distribution of the alleged rice infuriated the soldiers. On July 23, a military mutiny and riot broke out in <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>. Enraged soldiers headed for the residence of Min Gyeom-ho, who they had suspected of having swindled them out of their rice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Min, on hearing word of the revolt, ordered the police to arrest some of the ringleaders and announced that they would be executed the next morning. He had assumed that this would serve as a warning to the other agitators. However, after learning what had transpired, the rioters broke into Min's house to take vengeance; as he was not at his residence the rioters vented their frustrations by destroying his furniture and other possessions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The rioters then moved on to an armory from which they stole weapons and ammunition, and then headed for the prison. After overpowering the guards, they released not only the men who had been arrested that day by Min Gyeom-ho but also many political prisoners as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Min then summoned the army to quell the rebellion but it had become too late to suppress the mutiny. The original body of mutineers had been swelled by the poor and disaffected citizenry of the city, as a result the revolt had assumed major proportions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The rioters now turned their attention to the Japanese. One group headed to Lieutenant Horimoto's quarters and killed him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Another group, some 3,000 strong headed for the Japanese legation, where <a href="/wiki/Hanabusa_Yoshitada" title="Hanabusa Yoshitada">Hanabusa Yoshitada</a> the minister to Korea and twenty seven members of the legation resided.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The mob surrounded the legation shouting its intention of killing all the Japanese inside.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Hanabusa gave orders to burn the legation and important documents were set on fire. As the flames quickly spread, the members of the legation escaped through a rear gate, where they fled to the harbor and boarded a boat which took them down the <a href="/wiki/Han_River_(Korea)" title="Han River (Korea)">Han River</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chemulpo" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemulpo">Chemulpo</a>. Taking refuge with the Incheon commandant, they were again forced to flee after word arrived of the events in Seoul and the attitude of their hosts changed. They escaped to the harbor during heavy rain and were pursued by Korean soldiers. Six Japanese were killed, while another five were seriously wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373_18-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002373-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The survivors carrying the wounded, then boarded a small boat and headed for the open sea where three days later they were rescued by a British survey ship, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Flying_Fish_(1873)" title="HMS Flying Fish (1873)">HMS&#160;<i>Flying Fish</i></a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002374_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002374-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> which took them to <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>. The following day, after the attack on the Japanese legation, the rioters forced their way into the royal palace where they found and killed Min Gyeom-ho, as well as a dozen other high-ranking officers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002374_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002374-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> They also searched for Queen Min. The queen narrowly escaped, however, dressed as an ordinary lady of the court and was carried on the back of a faithful guard who claimed she was his sister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002374_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002374-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Daewongun used the incident to reassert his power.
</p><p>The Chinese then deployed about 4,500 troops to Korea, under General Wu Changqing, which effectively regained control and quelled the rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011236_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011236-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In response, the Japanese also sent four warships and a battalion of troops to Seoul to safeguard Japanese interests and demand reparations. However, tensions subsided with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Chemulpo" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Chemulpo">Treaty of Chemulpo</a>, signed on the evening of August 30, 1882. The agreement specified that the Korean conspirators would be punished and <a href="/wiki/Yen" class="mw-redirect" title="Yen">¥</a>50,000 would be paid to the families of slain Japanese. The Japanese government would also receive ¥500,000, a formal apology, and permission to station troops at their diplomatic legation in Seoul. In the aftermath of rebellion, the Daewongun was accused of fomenting the rebellion and its violence, and was arrested by Chinese and taken to Tianjin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002376_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002376-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> He was later carried off to a town about sixty miles southwest of Beijing, where for three years he was confined to one room and kept under strict surveillance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeene2002377_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeene2002377-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-assertion_of_Chinese_influence">Re-assertion of Chinese influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Re-assertion of Chinese influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the Imo Incident, early reform efforts in Korea suffered a major setback.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011237_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011237-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In the aftermath of the incident, the Chinese reasserted their influence over the peninsula, where they began to directly interfere in Korean internal affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011237_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011237-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> After stationing troops at strategic points in the capital Seoul, the Chinese undertook several initiatives to gain significant influence over the Korean government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012293Seth2011237_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012293Seth2011237-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Two special advisers on foreign affairs representing Chinese interests were dispatched to Korea: the German <a href="/wiki/Paul_Georg_von_M%C3%B6llendorff" title="Paul Georg von Möllendorff">Paul Georg von Möllendorff</a>, a close confidant of <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">Li Hongzhang</a>, and the Chinese diplomat <a href="/wiki/Ma_Jianzhong" title="Ma Jianzhong">Ma Jianzhong</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012293_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012293-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> A staff of Chinese officers also took over the training of the army, providing the Koreans with 1,000 rifles, two cannons, and 10,000 rounds of ammunition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199854_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199854-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the <i>Chingunyeong</i> (<i>Capital Guards Command</i>), a new Korean military formation, was created and trained along Chinese lines by <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shikai" title="Yuan Shikai">Yuan Shikai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012293_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012293-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October, the two countries signed a treaty stipulating that Korea was a dependency of China and granted Chinese merchants the right to conduct overland and maritime business freely within its borders. It also gave the Chinese substantial advantages over the Japanese and Westerners and also granted them unilateral extraterritoriality privileges in civil and criminal cases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuus199854_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuus199854-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Under the treaty the number of Chinese merchants and traders greatly increased, a severe blow to Korean merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012293_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012293-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Although it allowed Koreans reciprocally to trade in Beijing the agreement was not a treaty but was in effect issued as a regulation for a vassal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011237_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011237-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, during the following year, the Chinese supervised the creation of a Korean Maritime Customs Service, headed by von Möllendorff.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011237_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011237-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Korea was reduced to a semi-colonial tributary state of China with <a href="/wiki/Gojong_of_Korea" title="Gojong of Korea">King Gojong</a> unable to appoint diplomats without Chinese approval,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2002293_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2002293-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and with troops stationed in the country to protect Chinese interests.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Factional_rivalry_and_ascendancy_of_the_Min_clan">Factional rivalry and ascendancy of the Min clan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Factional rivalry and ascendancy of the Min clan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 1880s two rival factions emerged in Korea. One was a small group of reformers that had centered around the <i><a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_Party" title="Enlightenment Party">Gaehwadang</a></i>, (<i>Enlightenment Party</i>) which had become frustrated at the limited scale and arbitrary pace of reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011237_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011237-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The members who constituted the Enlightenment Party were youthful, well-educated Koreans and most were from the <a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">yangban</a> class.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011237_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011237-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> They were impressed by the developments in <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Meiji Japan">Meiji Japan</a> and were eager to emulate them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011237_28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011237-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Its members included <a href="/wiki/Kim_Ok-gyun" title="Kim Ok-gyun">Kim Ok-gyun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pak_Yung-hio" title="Pak Yung-hio">Pak Yung-hio</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hong_Yeong-sik&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hong Yeong-sik (page does not exist)">Hong Yeong-sik</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Seo_Gwang-beom&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Seo Gwang-beom (page does not exist)">Seo Gwang-beom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soh_Jaipil" title="Soh Jaipil">Soh Jaipil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011238_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011238-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The group was also relatively young; Pak Yung-hio came from a prestigious lineage related to the royal family, was 23, Hong was 29, Seo Gwang-beom was 25, and Soh Jaipil was 20; with Kim Ok-gyun being the oldest at 33.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011238_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011238-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> All had spent some time in Japan, Pak Yung-hio had been part of a mission sent to Japan to apologize for the Imo incident in 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011237_28-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011237-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> He had been accompanied by Seo Gwang-beom and by Kim Ok-gyun, who later come under the influence of Japanese modernizers such as <a href="/wiki/Fukuzawa_Yukichi" title="Fukuzawa Yukichi">Fukuzawa Yukichi</a>. Kim Ok-gyun, while studying in Japan, had also cultivated friendships with influential Japanese figures and became the de facto leader of the group.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011238_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011238-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> They were also strongly nationalistic and desired to make their country truly independent by ending Chinese interference in Korea's internal affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012293_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012293-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Sadaedang</i> was a group of conservatives, which included not only Min Yeong-ik from the Min family but also other prominent Korean political figures that wanted to maintain power with China's help. Although the members of the <i>Sadaedang</i> supported the enlightenment policy, they favored gradual changes based on the Chinese model.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012293_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012293-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> After the Imo incident, the Min clan pursued a pro-Chinese policy. This was also partly a matter of opportunism as the intervention by Chinese troops led to subsequent exile of the rival Daewongun in Tianjin and the expansion of Chinese influence in Korea, but it also reflected an ideological disposition also shared by many Koreans toward the more comfortable and traditional relationship as a tributary of China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011238_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011238-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, the Min clan became advocates of the <i>"dongdo seogi"</i> (<i>Adopting Western knowledge while keeping Eastern values</i>) philosophy, this had originated from the ideas of moderate Chinese reformers who had emphasized the need to maintain the perceived superior cultural values and heritage<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012289_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012289-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> of the Sino-centric world while recognizing the importance of acquiring and adopting Western technology, particularly military technology, in order to preserve autonomy. Hence, rather than the major institutional reforms such as the adaptation of new values such as legal equality or introducing modern education like in Meiji Japan, the advocates of this school of thought sought piecemeal adoptions of institutions that would strengthen the state while preserving the basic social, political, and cultural order.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011238_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011238-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Through the ascendancy of Queen Min to the throne, the Min clan had also been able to use the newly created institutions by the government as bases for political power, subsequently with their growing monopoly of key positions they frustrated the ambitions of the Enlightenment Party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2011238_34-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2011238-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gapsin_Coup">Gapsin Coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gapsin Coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gapsin_Coup" title="Gapsin Coup">Gapsin Coup</a></div>
<p>In the two years proceeding the Imo incident, the members of the <i>Gaehwadang</i> had failed to secure appointments to vital offices in the government and were unable to implement their reform plans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> As a consequence they were prepared to seize power by all means necessary. In 1884, an opportunity to seize power by staging a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d&#39;état">coup d'état</a> against the <i>Sadaedang</i> presented itself. In August, as hostilities between <a href="/wiki/Sino-French_War" title="Sino-French War">France and China</a> erupted over <a href="/wiki/Annam_(French_protectorate)" title="Annam (French protectorate)">Annam</a>, half of the Chinese troops stationed in Korea were withdrawn.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> On December 4, 1884, with the help of the Japanese minister Takezoe Shinichiro who promised to mobilize Japanese legation guards to provide assistance, the reformers staged their coup under the guise of a banquet hosted by Hong Yeong-sik, the director of the General Postal Administration. The banquet was to celebrate the opening of the new national post office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gojong_of_Korea" title="Gojong of Korea">King Gojong</a> was expected to attend together with several foreign diplomats and high-ranking officials, most of whom were members of the pro-Chinese <i>Sadaedang</i> faction. Kim Ok-gyun and his comrades approached King Gojong falsely stating that Chinese troops had created a disturbance and escorted him to the small Gyoengu Palace, where they placed him in the custody of Japanese legation guards. They then proceeded to kill and wound several senior officials of the <i>Sadaedang</i> faction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the coup, the <i>Gaehwadang</i> members formed a new government and devised a program of reform. The radical 14-point reform proposal stated that the following conditions be met: an end to Korea's tributary relationship with China; the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">ruling-class</a> privilege and the establishment of equal rights for all; the reorganization of the government as virtually a constitutional monarchy; the revision of land tax laws; cancellation of the grain loan system; the unification of all internal fiscal administrations under the jurisdiction of the Ho-jo; the suppression of privileged merchants and the development of free commerce and trade, the creation of a modern police system including police patrols and royal guards; and severe punishment of corrupt officials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the new government lasted no longer than a few days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Particularly, as the reformers were supported by no more than 140 Japanese troops who faced at least 1,500 Chinese garrisoned in Seoul,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294_35-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> under the command of General <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shikai" title="Yuan Shikai">Yuan Shikai</a>. With the reform measures being a threat to her clans' power, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Min" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Min">Queen Min</a> secretly requested military intervention from the Chinese. Consequently, within three days, even before the reform measures were made public, the coup was suppressed by the Chinese troops who attacked and defeated the Japanese forces and restored power to the pro-Chinese <i>Sadaedang</i> faction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294_35-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> During the ensuing melee Hong Yeong-sik was killed, the Japanese legation building was burned down and forty Japanese were killed. The surviving Korean coup leaders including Kim Ok-gyun escaped to the port of <a href="/wiki/Incheon" title="Incheon">Chemulpo</a> under escort of the Japanese minister Takezoe. From there they boarded a Japanese ship for exile in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012294Paine200359_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012294Paine200359-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1885, with a show of force the Japanese dispatched two battalions and seven warships to Korea,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012295_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012295-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> which resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Japan%E2%80%93Korea_Treaty_of_1885" title="Japan–Korea Treaty of 1885">Japan–Korea Treaty of 1885</a>, signed on 9 January 1885. The treaty restored <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Diplomatic relations">diplomatic relations</a> between Japan and Korea. The Koreans also agreed to pay the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Yen" class="mw-redirect" title="Yen">¥</a>100,000 for damages to their legation<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012295_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012295-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and to provide a site for the building of a new legation. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Ito_Hirobumi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ito Hirobumi">Ito Hirobumi</a>, in order to overcome Japan's disadvantageous position in Korea followed by the abortive coup, visited China to discuss the matter with his Chinese counterpart, <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">Li Hongzhang</a>. The two parties succeeded in concluding the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Tientsin" title="Convention of Tientsin">Convention of Tianjin</a> on May 31, 1885. They also pledged to withdraw their troops from Korea within four months, with prior notification to the other if troops were to be sent to Korea in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012295_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012295-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> After both countries withdrew their forces they left behind a precarious balance of power on the Korean Peninsula between the two nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012295_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012295-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, Yuan Shikai remained in Seoul, appointed as the Chinese Resident and continued to interfere with Korean domestic politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012295_37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012295-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The failure of the coup also marked a dramatic decline in Japanese influence over Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaine200359_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaine200359-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nagasaki_incident">Nagasaki incident</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nagasaki incident">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki_incident" title="Nagasaki incident">Nagasaki incident</a></div>
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2012</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki_incident" title="Nagasaki incident">Nagasaki incident</a> was a riot that took place in the Japanese port city of <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a> in 1886. Four warships from the Qing Empire's navy, the <a href="/wiki/Beiyang_Fleet" title="Beiyang Fleet">Beiyang Fleet</a>, stopped at Nagasaki, apparently to carry out repairs. Some Chinese sailors caused trouble in the city and started the riot. Several Japanese policemen confronting the rioters were killed. The Qing government did not apologize after the incident, which resulted in a wave of anti-Chinese sentiment in Japan.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bean_controversy">Bean controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bean controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A poor harvest in 1889 led the governor of Korea's <a href="/wiki/Hamgyong_Province" title="Hamgyong Province">Hamgyong Province</a> to prohibit <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybean</a> exports to Japan. Japan requested and received compensation in 1893 for their importers. The incident highlighted the growing dependence Japan felt on Korean food imports.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude_to_War">Prelude to War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Prelude to War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kim_Ok-gyun_affair">Kim Ok-gyun affair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kim Ok-gyun affair">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kim_Ok-gyun.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kim_Ok-gyun.gif/150px-Kim_Ok-gyun.gif" decoding="async" width="150" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kim_Ok-gyun.gif/225px-Kim_Ok-gyun.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/69/Kim_Ok-gyun.gif 2x" data-file-width="291" data-file-height="434" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kim_Ok-gyun.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kim_Ok-gyun" title="Kim Ok-gyun">Kim Ok-gyun</a> photographed in Nagasaki in 1882. His assassination in China would contribute to tensions leading to the First Sino-Japanese War.</div></div></div>
<p>On March 28, 1894, a pro-Japanese Korean revolutionary, <a href="/wiki/Kim_Ok-gyun" title="Kim Ok-gyun">Kim Ok-gyun</a>, was assassinated in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>. Kim had fled to Japan after his involvement in the 1884 coup and the Japanese had turned down Korean demands that he be extradited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2002431_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2002431-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Many Japanese activists saw in him potential for a future role in Korean modernization, however, Meiji government leaders were more cautious; after some reservations they exiled him to the <a href="/wiki/Bonin_Islands" title="Bonin Islands">Bonin (Ogasawara) Islands</a>. Ultimately, he was lured to Shanghai, where he was killed by a Korean, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Jong-u" title="Hong Jong-u">Hong Jong-u</a>, in his room at a Japanese inn in the <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_International_Settlement" title="Shanghai International Settlement">international settlement</a>. After some hesitation, the British authorities in Shanghai concluded that rules against extradition did not apply to a corpse and turned his body over to Chinese authorities. His body was then taken aboard a Chinese warship and sent back to Korea, where it was cut up, quartered and displayed in all Korean provinces as a warning to other purported rebels and traitors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2002431_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2002431-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Tokyo, the Japanese government took this as an outrageous affront.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2002431_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2002431-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Kim Ok-gyun's brutal murder was portrayed as a betrayal by Li Hongzhang and a setback for Japan's stature and dignity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2002431_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2002431-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Not only did the Chinese authorities refuse to press charges against the assassin, but he was even allowed to accompany Kim's mutilated body back to Korea, where he was showered with rewards and honors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElleman200196_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElleman200196-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Kim's assassination had also called Japan's commitment to its Korean supporters into question. The police in Tokyo had foiled an earlier attempt during the same year to assassinate <a href="/wiki/Pak_Yung-hio" title="Pak Yung-hio">Pak Yung-hio</a>, one of the other Korean leaders of the 1884 uprising. When two suspected Korean assassins received asylum at the Korean legation, it had also instigated a diplomatic outrage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElleman200196_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElleman200196-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Although the Japanese government could have immediately used Kim's assassination to its advantage, it concluded that since Kim died on Chinese territory the treatment of the corpse was outside its authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElleman200196_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElleman200196-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> But the shocking murder of the Korean inflamed Japanese opinion, many in the country considered the Chinese supported actions as also being directed against Japan. To the Japanese, the Chinese had also showed their contempt for <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>, when they set free the suspected assassin, who had been arrested by British authorities in Shanghai and then in accordance with treaty obligations turned over to the Chinese for trial. Nationalistic groups immediately began to call for war with China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElleman200196_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElleman200196-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Donghak_Rebellion">Donghak Rebellion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Donghak Rebellion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Donghak_Peasant_Revolution" title="Donghak Peasant Revolution">Donghak Peasant Revolution</a></div>
<p>Tension ran high between China and Japan by June 1894 but war was not yet inevitable. On June 4, the Korean king, <a href="/wiki/Gojong_of_Korea" title="Gojong of Korea">Gojong</a>, requested aid from the Qing government in suppressing the <a href="/wiki/Donghak_Peasant_Revolution" title="Donghak Peasant Revolution">Donghak Rebellion</a>. Although the rebellion was not as serious as it initially seemed and hence Qing reinforcements were not necessary, the Qing government still sent the general <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shikai" title="Yuan Shikai">Yuan Shikai</a> as its <a href="/wiki/Plenipotentiary" title="Plenipotentiary">plenipotentiary</a> to lead 2,800 troops to Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> According to the Japanese, the Qing government had violated the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Tientsin" title="Convention of Tientsin">Convention of Tientsin</a> by not informing the Japanese government of its decision to send troops, but the Qing claimed that Japan had approved this.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese countered by sending an 8,000-troop expeditionary force (the Oshima Composite Brigade) to Korea. The first 400 troops arrived on June 9 en route to <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, and 3,000 landed at <a href="/wiki/Incheon" title="Incheon">Incheon</a> on June 12.<sup id="cite_ref-Seth_2010_225_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seth_2010_225-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Japanese officials denied any intention to intervene. As a result, the Qing viceroy <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">Li Hongzhang</a> "was lured into believing that Japan would not wage war, but the Japanese were fully prepared to act".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Attribution_needed" title="Wikipedia:Attribution needed"><span title="This sentence may contain perspectives and opinions without stating whose they are. (November 2016)">attribution needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKwang-Ching1978105_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKwang-Ching1978105-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The Qing government turned down Japan's suggestion for Japan and China to cooperate to reform the Korean government. When Korea demanded that Japan withdraw its troops from Korea, the Japanese refused.
</p><p>In early June 1894, the 8,000 Japanese troops captured the Korean king Gojong, occupied the <a href="/wiki/Gyeongbokgung" title="Gyeongbokgung">Gyeongbokgung</a> in Seoul and, by June 25, replaced the existing Korean government with members of the pro-Japanese faction.<sup id="cite_ref-Seth_2010_225_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seth_2010_225-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Even though Qing forces were already leaving Korea after finding themselves unneeded there, the new pro-Japanese Korean government granted Japan the right to expel Qing forces while Japan dispatched more troops to Korea. The Qing Empire rejected the new Korean government as illegitimate.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_combatants">Status of combatants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Status of combatants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Japanese reforms under the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Meiji_Japan" title="Government of Meiji Japan">Meiji government</a> gave significant priority to the creation of an effective <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army" title="Imperial Japanese Army">modern national army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">navy</a>, especially naval construction. Japan sent numerous military officials abroad for training and evaluation of the relative strengths and tactics of Western armies and navies.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_Japanese_Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=First_Sino-Japanese_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Imperial Japanese Navy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Itoh_Sukeyuki.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Itoh_Sukeyuki.jpg/200px-Itoh_Sukeyuki.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Itoh_Sukeyuki.jpg/300px-Itoh_Sukeyuki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Itoh_Sukeyuki.jpg/400px-Itoh_Sukeyuki.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Itoh_Sukeyuki.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/It%C5%8D_Sukeyuki" title="Itō Sukeyuki">Itō Sukeyuki</a>, Commander-in-Chief of the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Combined_Fleet" title="Combined Fleet">Combined Fleet</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Matsushima(Bertin).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Matsushima%28Bertin%29.jpg/200px-Matsushima%28Bertin%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Matsushima%28Bertin%29.jpg/300px-Matsushima%28Bertin%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Matsushima%28Bertin%29.jpg/400px-Matsushima%28Bertin%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Matsushima(Bertin).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The French-built <i><a href="/wiki/Japanese_cruiser_Matsushima" title="Japanese cruiser Matsushima">Matsushima</a></i>, flagship of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</a> during the Sino-Japanese conflict</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</a> was modeled after the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvansPeattie199712_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvansPeattie199712-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> at the time the foremost naval power. British advisors were sent to Japan to train the naval establishment, while Japanese students were in turn sent to Britain to study and observe the Royal Navy. Through drilling and tuition by Royal Navy instructors, Japan developed naval officers expert in the arts of gunnery and seamanship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvansPeattie199736_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvansPeattie199736-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> At the start of hostilities, the Imperial Japanese Navy comprised a fleet of 12 modern warships, (the <a href="/wiki/Protected_cruiser" title="Protected cruiser">protected cruiser</a> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cruiser_Izumi" title="Japanese cruiser Izumi"><i>Izumi</i></a> being added during the war), eight <a href="/wiki/Corvette" title="Corvette">corvettes</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Ironclad_warship" title="Ironclad warship">ironclad warship</a>, 26 <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_boat" title="Torpedo boat">torpedo boats</a>, and numerous <a href="/wiki/Armed_merchantmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed merchantmen">auxiliary/armed merchant cruisers</a> and converted <a href="/wiki/Ocean_liner" title="Ocean liner">liners</a>. During peacetime, the warships of the Imperial Japanese Navy were divided among three main naval bases at <a href="/wiki/Yokosuka" title="Yokosuka">Yokosuka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kure" class="mw-redirect" title="Kure">Kure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sasebo,_Nagasaki" title="Sasebo, Nagasaki">Sasebo</a> and following mobilization, the navy was composed of five divisions of seagoing warships and three flotillas of torpedo boats with a fourth being formed at the beginning of hostilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlender201439_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlender201439-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese also had a relatively large <a href="/wiki/Merchant_navy" title="Merchant navy">merchant navy</a>, which at the beginning of 1894 consisted of 288 vessels. Of these, 66 belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Yusen_Kaisha" class="mw-redirect" title="Nippon Yusen Kaisha">Nippon Yusen Kaisha</a> shipping company, which received national subsidies from the Japanese government to maintain the vessels for use by the navy in time of war. As a consequence, the navy could call on a sufficient number of <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_ship" title="Auxiliary ship">auxiliaries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Troopship" title="Troopship">transports</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlender201439_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlender201439-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Japan did not yet have the resources to acquire <a href="/wiki/Battleships" class="mw-redirect" title="Battleships">battleships</a> and so planned to employ the <i><a href="/wiki/Jeune_%C3%89cole" title="Jeune École">Jeune École</a></i> doctrine, which favoured small, fast warships, especially <a href="/wiki/Cruiser" title="Cruiser">cruisers</a> and torpedo boats, with the offensive capability to destroy larger craft. The Japanese naval leadership, on the eve of hostilities, was generally cautious and even apprehensive,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvansPeattie199738_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvansPeattie199738-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> as the navy had not yet received the warships ordered in February 1893, particularly the battleships <a href="/wiki/Japanese_battleship_Fuji" title="Japanese battleship Fuji"><i>Fuji</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_battleship_Yashima" title="Japanese battleship Yashima"><i>Yashima</i></a> and the protected cruiser <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cruiser_Akashi" title="Japanese cruiser Akashi"><i>Akashi</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchencking200581_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchencking200581-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Hence, initiating hostilities at the time was not ideal, and the navy was far less confident than the army about the outcome of a war with China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvansPeattie199738_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvansPeattie199738-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of Japan's major warships were built in British and French shipyards (eight British, three French and two Japanese-built) and 16 of the torpedo boats were known to have been built in France and assembled in Japan.
</p>
<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="Major_Japanese_naval_combatants_of_the_First_Sino-Japanese_War_30px&amp;#124;Japanese_Navy_Ensign" style="padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks mw-collapsible mw-collapsed navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:IJNOrder1894-95" title="Template:IJNOrder1894-95"><abbr title="View this template" style=";;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none;
